   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Climate change
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>

    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      For current and future climatological effects of human influences, see
      <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
       global warming
      </a>
      . For the study of past climate change, see
      <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">
       paleoclimatology
      </a>
      . For temperatures on the longest time scales, see
      <a href="/wiki/Geologic_temperature_record" title="Geologic temperature record">
       geologic temperature record
      </a>
      .

     <p>
      <b>
       Climate change
      </b>
      is a change in the statistical distribution of
      <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">
       weather
      </a>
      patterns when that change lasts for an extended period of time (i.e., decades to millions of years). Climate change may refer to a change in average weather conditions, or in the time variation of weather around longer-term average conditions (i.e., more or fewer
      <a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">
       extreme weather
      </a>
      events). Climate change is caused by factors such as
      <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/biotic" title="wikt:biotic">
       biotic
      </a>
      processes, variations in
      <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">
       solar radiation
      </a>
      received by Earth,
      <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">
       plate tectonics
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanic_eruptions" title="Volcanic eruptions">
       volcanic eruptions
      </a>
      . Certain human activities have also been identified as significant causes of recent climate change, often referred to as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
        global warming
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">
       Scientists
      </a>
      actively work to understand past and future
      <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">
       climate
      </a>
      by using
      <a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">
       observations
      </a>
      and theoretical models. A climate record—extending deep into the Earth's past—has been assembled, and continues to be built up, based on geological evidence from
      <a href="/wiki/Borehole" title="Borehole">
       borehole
      </a>
      temperature profiles,
      <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">
       cores removed from deep accumulations of ice
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">
       floral
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">
       faunal
      </a>
      records, glacial and
      <a href="/wiki/Periglaciation" title="Periglaciation">
       periglacial
      </a>
      processes, stable-isotope and other analyses of sediment layers, and records of past sea levels. More recent data are provided by the instrumental record.
      <a href="/wiki/General_circulation_model" title="General circulation model">
       General circulation models
      </a>
      , based on the
      <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_physical_science" title="Outline of physical science">
       physical sciences
      </a>
      , are often used in theoretical approaches to match past climate data, make future projections, and link causes and effects in climate change.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">
       Terminology
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Global_warming#Etymology" title="Global warming">
       Global warming § Etymology
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The most general definition of
      <i>
       climate change
      </i>
      is a change in the statistical properties (principally its
      <a href="/wiki/Central_tendency" title="Central tendency">
       mean
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statistical_variability" title="Statistical variability">
       spread
      </a>
      )
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      of the climate system when considered over long periods of time, regardless of cause.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Accordingly, fluctuations over periods shorter than a few decades, such as
      <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">
       El Niño
      </a>
      , do not represent climate change.
     </p>
     <p>
      The term sometimes is used to refer specifically to climate change caused by human activity, as opposed to changes in climate that may have resulted as part of Earth's natural processes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In this sense, especially in the context of
      <a href="/wiki/Environmental_policy" title="Environmental policy">
       environmental policy
      </a>
      , the term
      <i>
       climate change
      </i>
      has become synonymous with
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">
        anthropogenic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
        global warming
       </a>
      </i>
      . Within scientific journals,
      <i>
       global warming
      </i>
      refers to surface temperature increases while
      <i>
       climate change
      </i>
      includes global warming and everything else that increasing
      <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">
       greenhouse gas
      </a>
      levels affect.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Climatic_change_versus_climate_change">
       Climatic change versus climate change
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In 1966, the
      <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">
       World Meteorological Organization
      </a>
      (WMO) proposed the term climatic change to encompass all forms of climatic variability on time-scales longer than 10 years, whether the cause was natural or anthropogenic. Change was a given and climatic was used as an adjective to describe this kind of change (as opposed to political or economic change). When it was realized that human activities had a potential to drastically alter the climate, the term climate change replaced climatic change as the dominant term to reflect an anthropogenic cause. Climate change was incorporated in the title of the
      <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">
       Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change
      </a>
      (IPCC) and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="UN Framework Convention on Climate Change">
       UN Framework Convention on Climate Change
      </a>
      (UNFCCC). Climate change, used as a noun, became an issue rather than the technical description of changing weather.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">
       Causes
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      On the broadest scale, the rate at which energy is received from the
      <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
       Sun
      </a>
      and the rate at which it is lost to space determine the equilibrium temperature and climate of Earth. This energy is distributed around the globe by winds, ocean currents, and other mechanisms to affect the climates of different regions.
     </p>
     <p>
      Factors that can shape climate are called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_forcing" title="Climate forcing">
       climate forcings
      </a>
      or "forcing mechanisms".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA-Glossary_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-EPA-Glossary-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      These include processes such as variations in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">
       solar radiation
      </a>
      , variations in the Earth's orbit, variations in the
      <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
       albedo
      </a>
      or reflectivity of the continents and oceans,
      <a href="/wiki/Orogeny" title="Orogeny">
       mountain-building
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Continental_drift" title="Continental drift">
       continental drift
      </a>
      and changes in
      <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">
       greenhouse gas
      </a>
      concentrations. There are a variety of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_feedback" title="Climate feedback">
       climate change feedbacks
      </a>
      that can either amplify or diminish the initial forcing. Some parts of the climate system, such as the oceans and ice caps, respond more slowly in reaction to climate forcings, while others respond more quickly. There are also key
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_threshold" title="Ecological threshold">
       threshold factors
      </a>
      which when exceeded can produce rapid change.
     </p>
     <p>
      Forcing mechanisms can be either "internal" or "external". Internal forcing mechanisms are natural processes within the climate system itself (e.g., the
      <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">
       thermohaline circulation
      </a>
      ). External forcing mechanisms can be either natural (e.g., changes in solar output) or anthropogenic (e.g., increased emissions of greenhouse gases).
     </p>
     <p>
      Whether the initial forcing mechanism is internal or external, the response of the climate system might be fast (e.g., a sudden cooling due to airborne
      <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_ash" title="Volcanic ash">
       volcanic ash
      </a>
      reflecting sunlight), slow (e.g.
      <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">
       thermal expansion
      </a>
      of warming ocean water), or a combination (e.g., sudden loss of
      <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
       albedo
      </a>
      in the arctic ocean as sea ice melts, followed by more gradual thermal expansion of the water). Therefore, the climate system can respond abruptly, but the full response to forcing mechanisms might not be fully developed for centuries or even longer.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_forcing_mechanisms">
       Internal forcing mechanisms
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Scientists generally define the five components of earth's climate system to include
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_Atmosphere" title="Earth's Atmosphere">
       atmosphere
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">
       hydrosphere
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cryosphere" title="Cryosphere">
       cryosphere
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">
       lithosphere
      </a>
      (restricted to the surface soils, rocks, and sediments), and
      <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">
       biosphere
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Natural changes in the climate system ("internal forcings") result in internal "climate variability".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Examples include the type and distribution of species, and changes in ocean currents.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ocean_variability">
       Ocean variability
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">
       Thermohaline circulation
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Climate_inertia" title="Climate inertia">
       Climate inertia
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ecinfigtwo.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="410" data-file-width="943" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Ecinfigtwo.jpg/220px-Ecinfigtwo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Ecinfigtwo.jpg/330px-Ecinfigtwo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Ecinfigtwo.jpg/440px-Ecinfigtwo.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ecinfigtwo.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Decadal_Oscillation" title="Pacific Decadal Oscillation">
         Pacific Decadal Oscillation
        </a>
        1925 to 2010
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The ocean is a fundamental part of the climate system, some changes in it occurring at longer timescales than in the
      <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">
       atmosphere
      </a>
      , as it has hundreds of times more mass and thus very high
      <a href="/wiki/Volumetric_heat_capacity" title="Volumetric heat capacity">
       thermal inertia
      </a>
      , with effects such as the ocean depths still lagging today in temperature adjustment from effects of the
      <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">
       Little Ice Age
      </a>
      of past centuries).
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
         <span title="Had an HTML comment &quot;THIS SENTENCE MAKES NO SENSE&quot; that couldn't be readily dated, though it was apparently after March 2011. I can't speak for the original tagger, but I agree the statement is confusing. -BDD (January 2013)">
          clarification needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Short-term fluctuations (years to a few decades) such as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o-Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño-Southern Oscillation">
       El Niño-Southern Oscillation
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Pacific_decadal_oscillation" title="Pacific decadal oscillation">
       Pacific decadal oscillation
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_oscillation" title="North Atlantic oscillation">
       North Atlantic oscillation
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Arctic_oscillation" title="Arctic oscillation">
       Arctic oscillation
      </a>
      , represent
      <b>
       climate variability
      </b>
      rather than climate change. On longer time-scales, alterations to ocean processes such as thermohaline circulation play a key role in redistributing heat by carrying out a very slow and extremely deep movement of water and the long-term redistribution of heat in the world's oceans.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ocean_circulation_conveyor_belt.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="476" data-file-width="730" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Ocean_circulation_conveyor_belt.jpg/220px-Ocean_circulation_conveyor_belt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Ocean_circulation_conveyor_belt.jpg/330px-Ocean_circulation_conveyor_belt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Ocean_circulation_conveyor_belt.jpg/440px-Ocean_circulation_conveyor_belt.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ocean_circulation_conveyor_belt.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A schematic of modern
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermohaline" title="Thermohaline">
         thermohaline
        </a>
        circulation. Tens of millions of years ago, continental-plate movement formed a land-free gap around Antarctica, allowing the formation of the
        <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current" title="Antarctic Circumpolar Current">
         ACC
        </a>
        , which keeps warm waters away from Antarctica.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Life">
       Life
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Life affects climate through its role in the
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">
       carbon
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">
       water cycles
      </a>
      and through such mechanisms as
      <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
       albedo
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">
       evapotranspiration
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">
       cloud formation
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">
       weathering
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Examples of how life may have affected past climate include:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciation" title="Glaciation">
        glaciation
       </a>
       2.3 billion years ago triggered by the evolution of oxygenic
       <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">
        photosynthesis
       </a>
       , which depleted the atmosphere of the greenhouse gas carbon dioxide and introduced free oxygen.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       another glaciation 300 million years ago ushered in by long-term burial of
       <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">
        decomposition-resistant
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Detritus" title="Detritus">
        detritus
       </a>
       of vascular land-plants (creating a
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">
        carbon sink
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Coal#Formation" title="Coal">
        forming coal
       </a>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       termination of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleocene-Eocene_Thermal_Maximum" title="Paleocene-Eocene Thermal Maximum">
        Paleocene-Eocene Thermal Maximum
       </a>
       55 million years ago by flourishing marine
       <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">
        phytoplankton
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zachos1999_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Zachos1999-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       reversal of global warming 49 million years ago by
       <a href="/wiki/Azolla_event" title="Azolla event">
        800,000 years of arctic azolla blooms
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Global_cooling" title="Global cooling">
        global cooling
       </a>
       over the past 40 million years driven by the expansion of grass-grazer
       <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">
        ecosystems
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="External_forcing_mechanisms">
       External forcing mechanisms
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MilankovitchCyclesOrbitandCores.png">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="1044" data-file-width="1000" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/MilankovitchCyclesOrbitandCores.png/220px-MilankovitchCyclesOrbitandCores.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/MilankovitchCyclesOrbitandCores.png/330px-MilankovitchCyclesOrbitandCores.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/MilankovitchCyclesOrbitandCores.png/440px-MilankovitchCyclesOrbitandCores.png 2x" width="220"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Milankovitch cycles from 800,000 years ago in the past to 800,000 years in the future.
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vostok_Petit_data.svg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vostok_Petit_data.svg/220px-Vostok_Petit_data.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vostok_Petit_data.svg/330px-Vostok_Petit_data.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vostok_Petit_data.svg/440px-Vostok_Petit_data.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Variations in
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CO2" title="CO2">
          CO
          <sub>
           2
          </sub>
         </a>
         , temperature and dust from the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vostok,_Antarctica" title="Vostok, Antarctica">
          Vostok
         </a>
         ice core over the last 450,000 years
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_variations">
       Orbital variations
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles" title="Milankovitch cycles">
       Milankovitch cycles
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Slight variations in Earth's orbit lead to changes in the seasonal distribution of sunlight reaching the Earth's surface and how it is distributed across the globe. There is very little change to the area-averaged annually averaged sunshine; but there can be strong changes in the geographical and seasonal distribution. The three types of orbital variations are variations in Earth's
      <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">
       eccentricity
      </a>
      , changes in
      <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">
       the tilt angle of Earth's axis of rotation
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">
       precession
      </a>
      of Earth's axis. Combined together, these produce
      <a href="/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles" title="Milankovitch cycles">
       Milankovitch cycles
      </a>
      which have a large impact on climate and are notable for their correlation to
      <a href="/wiki/Glacial_period" title="Glacial period">
       glacial
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interglacial_period" title="Interglacial period">
       interglacial periods
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msu_milankovitch_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-msu_milankovitch-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
      their correlation with the advance and retreat of the
      <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">
       Sahara
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msu_milankovitch_24-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-msu_milankovitch-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and for their
      <a href="/wiki/Cyclostratigraphy" title="Cyclostratigraphy">
       appearance
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Geologic_record" title="Geologic record">
       stratigraphic record
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPCC" title="IPCC">
       IPCC
      </a>
      notes that Milankovitch cycles drove the ice age cycles,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CO2" title="CO2">
       CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
      </a>
      followed temperature change "with a lag of some hundreds of years," and that as a feedback amplified temperature change.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The depths of the ocean have a lag time in changing temperature (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_inertia" title="Thermal inertia">
       thermal inertia
      </a>
      on such scale). Upon seawater temperature change, the solubility of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      in the oceans changed, as well as other factors impacting air-sea CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      exchange.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_output">
       Solar output
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_variation" title="Solar variation">
       Solar variation
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_Activity_Proxies.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="361" data-file-width="559" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Solar_Activity_Proxies.png/220px-Solar_Activity_Proxies.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Solar_Activity_Proxies.png/330px-Solar_Activity_Proxies.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Solar_Activity_Proxies.png/440px-Solar_Activity_Proxies.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_Activity_Proxies.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Variations in solar activity during the last several centuries based on observations of
        <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">
         sunspots
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">
         beryllium
        </a>
        isotopes. The period of extraordinarily few sunspots in the late 17th century was the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maunder_minimum" title="Maunder minimum">
         Maunder minimum
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
       Sun
      </a>
      is the predominant source of
      <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
       energy
      </a>
      input to the Earth. Other sources include
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geothermal" title="Geothermal">
       geothermal
      </a>
      energy from the Earth's core, and heat from the decay of radioactive compounds. Both long- and short-term variations in solar intensity are known to affect global climate.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Earth" title="History of the Earth">
       Three to four billion years ago
      </a>
      , the Sun emitted only 70% as much power as it does today. If the atmospheric composition had been the same as today, liquid water should not have existed on Earth. However, there is evidence for the presence of water on the early Earth, in the
      <a href="/wiki/Hadean" title="Hadean">
       Hadean
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty.2C_B._2006_421_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Marty.2C_B._2006_421-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Archean" title="Archean">
       Archean
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty.2C_B._2006_421_29-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Marty.2C_B._2006_421-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      eons, leading to what is known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Faint_young_Sun_paradox" title="Faint young Sun paradox">
       faint young Sun paradox
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sagan1972_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sagan1972-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hypothesized solutions to this paradox include a vastly different atmosphere, with much higher concentrations of greenhouse gases than currently exist.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Over the following approximately 4 billion years, the energy output of the Sun increased and atmospheric composition changed. The
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Oxygenation_Event" title="Great Oxygenation Event">
       Great Oxygenation Event
      </a>
      – oxygenation of the atmosphere around 2.4 billion years ago – was the most notable alteration. Over the next five billion years, the Sun's ultimate death as it becomes a
      <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">
       red giant
      </a>
      and then a
      <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">
       white dwarf
      </a>
      will have large effects on climate, with the red giant phase possibly ending any life on Earth that survives until that time.
     </p>
     <p>
      Solar output also
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_variation" title="Solar variation">
       varies
      </a>
      on shorter time scales, including the 11-year
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_cycle" title="Solar cycle">
       solar cycle
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and longer-term
      <a href="/wiki/Modulation" title="Modulation">
       modulations
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Solar intensity variations possibly as a result of the Wolf,
      <a href="/wiki/Sp%C3%B6rer_Minimum" title="Spörer Minimum">
       Spörer
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Maunder_Minimum" title="Maunder Minimum">
       Maunder Minimum
      </a>
      are considered to have been influential in triggering the Little Ice Age,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and some of the warming observed from 1900 to 1950. The cyclical nature of the Sun's energy output is not yet fully understood; it differs from the very slow change that is happening within the Sun as it ages and evolves. Research indicates that solar variability has had effects including the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maunder_minimum" title="Maunder minimum">
       Maunder minimum
      </a>
      from 1645 to 1715
      <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">
       A.D.
      </a>
      , part of the Little Ice Age from 1550 to 1850 A.D. that was marked by relative cooling and greater glacier extent than the centuries before and afterward.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Little_Ice_Age_definition_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Little_Ice_Age_definition-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some studies point toward solar radiation increases from cyclical sunspot activity affecting global warming, and climate may be influenced by the sum of all effects (solar variation, anthropogenic
      <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">
       radiative forcings
      </a>
      , etc.).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goddard_Space_Flight_Center_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Goddard_Space_Flight_Center-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Interestingly, a 2010 study
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       suggests
      </i>
      , “that the effects of solar variability on temperature throughout the atmosphere may be contrary to current expectations.”
     </p>
     <p>
      In an Aug 2011 Press Release,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/CERN" title="CERN">
       CERN
      </a>
      announced the publication in the
      <a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">
       Nature
      </a>
      journal the initial results from its
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CLOUD" title="CLOUD">
       CLOUD
      </a>
      experiment. The results indicate that ionisation from cosmic rays significantly enhances aerosol formation in the presence of sulfuric acid and water, but in the lower atmosphere where ammonia is also required, this is insufficient to account for aerosol formation and additional trace vapours must be involved. The next step is to find more about these trace vapours, including whether they are of natural or human origin.
     </p>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray#Postulated_role_in_climate_change" title="Cosmic ray">
       Cosmic ray § Postulated role in climate change
      </a>
     </div>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Volcanism">
       Volcanism
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Msu_1978-2010.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="244" data-file-width="544" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Msu_1978-2010.jpg/220px-Msu_1978-2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Msu_1978-2010.jpg/330px-Msu_1978-2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Msu_1978-2010.jpg/440px-Msu_1978-2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Msu_1978-2010.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        In atmospheric temperature from 1979 to 2010, determined by
        <a href="/wiki/Microwave_sounding_unit" title="Microwave sounding unit">
         MSU
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
         NASA
        </a>
        satellites, effects appear from
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerosols" title="Aerosols">
         aerosols
        </a>
        released by major volcanic eruptions (
        <a href="/wiki/El_Chich%C3%B3n" title="El Chichón">
         El Chichón
        </a>
        and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pinatubo" title="Pinatubo">
         Pinatubo
        </a>
        ).
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o-Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño-Southern Oscillation">
         El Niño
        </a>
        is a separate event, from ocean variability.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">
       eruptions
      </a>
      considered to be large enough to affect the Earth's climate on a scale of more than 1 year are the ones that inject over 100,000
      <a href="/wiki/Ton" title="Ton">
       tons
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">
       SO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
      </a>
      into the
      <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">
       stratosphere
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2004miles_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-2004miles-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This is due to the optical properties of SO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      and sulfate aerosols, which strongly absorb or scatter solar radiation, creating a global layer of
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">
       sulfuric acid
      </a>
      haze.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On average, such eruptions occur several times per century, and cause cooling (by partially blocking the transmission of solar radiation to the Earth's surface) for a period of a few years.
     </p>
     <p>
      The eruption of
      <a href="/wiki/Mount_Pinatubo" title="Mount Pinatubo">
       Mount Pinatubo
      </a>
      in 1991, the second largest terrestrial eruption of the 20th century, affected the climate substantially, subsequently global temperatures decreased by about 0.5 °C (0.9 °F) for up to three years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thus, the cooling over large parts of the Earth reduced surface temperatures in 1991-93, the equivalent to a reduction in net radiation of 4 watts per square meter.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nasa-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Mount_Tambora" title="Mount Tambora">
       Mount Tambora
      </a>
      eruption in 1815 caused the
      <a href="/wiki/Year_Without_a_Summer" title="Year Without a Summer">
       Year Without a Summer
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Much larger eruptions, known as
      <a href="/wiki/Large_igneous_province" title="Large igneous province">
       large igneous provinces
      </a>
      , occur only a few times every fifty - hundred million years - through
      <a href="/wiki/Flood_basalt" title="Flood basalt">
       flood basalt
      </a>
      , and caused in Earth past global warming and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">
       mass extinctions
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Small eruptions, with injections of less than 0.1 Mt of sulfur dioxide into the stratosphere, impact the atmosphere only subtly, as temperature changes are comparable with natural variability. However, because smaller eruptions occur at a much higher frequency, they too have a significant impact on Earth's atmosphere.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2004miles_43-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-2004miles-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1997graf_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-1997graf-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Prediction_of_volcanic_activity" title="Prediction of volcanic activity">
       Seismic monitoring
      </a>
      maps current and future trends in volcanic activities, and tries to develop early warning systems. In
      <a href="/wiki/Climate_model" title="Climate model">
       climate modelling
      </a>
      the aim is to study the physical mechanisms and feedbacks of volcanic forcing.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Volcanoes are also part of the extended
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">
       carbon cycle
      </a>
      . Over very long (geological) time periods, they release carbon dioxide from the Earth's crust and mantle, counteracting the uptake by sedimentary rocks and other geological
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_sink" title="Carbon dioxide sink">
       carbon dioxide sinks
      </a>
      . The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Geological_Survey" title="US Geological Survey">
       US Geological Survey
      </a>
      estimates are that volcanic emissions are at a much lower level than the effects of current human activities, which generate 100–300 times the amount of carbon dioxide emitted by volcanoes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A review of published studies indicates that annual volcanic emissions of carbon dioxide, including amounts released from mid-ocean ridges, volcanic arcs, and hot spot volcanoes, are only the equivalent of 3 to 5 days of human caused output. The annual amount put out by human activities may be greater than the amount released by
      <a href="/wiki/Supervolcano" title="Supervolcano">
       supererruptions
      </a>
      , the most recent of which was the
      <a href="/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory" title="Toba catastrophe theory">
       Toba eruption
      </a>
      in Indonesia 74,000 years ago.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.agu.org_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-www.agu.org-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although volcanoes are technically part of the lithosphere, which itself is part of the climate system, the IPCC explicitly defines volcanism as an external forcing agent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Plate_tectonics">
       Plate tectonics
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">
       Plate tectonics
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Over the course of millions of years, the motion of tectonic plates reconfigures global land and ocean areas and generates topography. This can affect both global and local patterns of climate and atmosphere-ocean circulation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The position of the continents determines the geometry of the oceans and therefore influences patterns of ocean circulation. The locations of the seas are important in controlling the transfer of heat and moisture across the globe, and therefore, in determining global climate. A recent example of tectonic control on ocean circulation is the formation of the
      <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Panama" title="Isthmus of Panama">
       Isthmus of Panama
      </a>
      about 5 million years ago, which shut off direct mixing between the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic" title="Atlantic">
       Atlantic
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific" title="Pacific">
       Pacific
      </a>
      Oceans. This strongly affected the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_boundary_current" title="Western boundary current">
       ocean dynamics
      </a>
      of what is now the
      <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">
       Gulf Stream
      </a>
      and may have led to Northern Hemisphere ice cover.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      During the
      <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">
       Carboniferous
      </a>
      period, about 300 to 360 million years ago, plate tectonics may have triggered large-scale storage of carbon and increased
      <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/glaciation" title="wikt:glaciation">
       glaciation
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Geologic evidence points to a "megamonsoonal" circulation pattern during the time of the
      <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">
       supercontinent
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">
       Pangaea
      </a>
      , and climate modeling suggests that the existence of the supercontinent was conducive to the establishment of monsoons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The size of continents is also important. Because of the stabilizing effect of the oceans on temperature, yearly temperature variations are generally lower in coastal areas than they are inland. A larger supercontinent will therefore have more area in which climate is strongly seasonal than will several smaller continents or
      <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">
       islands
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Human_influences">
       Human influences
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="708" data-file-width="708" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg/220px-Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg/330px-Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg/440px-Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Increase in atmospheric CO
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
        levels
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
       Global warming
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In the context of climate variation, anthropogenic factors are human activities which affect the climate. The
      <a href="/wiki/Scientific_opinion_on_climate_change" title="Scientific opinion on climate change">
       scientific consensus on climate change
      </a>
      is "that climate is changing and that these changes are in large part caused by human activities,"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and it "is largely irreversible."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       “Science has made enormous inroads in understanding climate change and its causes, and is beginning to help develop a strong understanding of current and potential impacts that will affect people today and in coming decades. This understanding is crucial because it allows decision makers to place climate change in the context of other large challenges facing the nation and the world. There are still some uncertainties, and there always will be in understanding a complex system like Earth’s climate. Nevertheless, there is a strong, credible body of evidence, based on multiple lines of research, documenting that climate is changing and that these changes are in large part caused by human activities. While much remains to be learned, the core phenomenon, scientific questions, and hypotheses have been examined thoroughly and have stood firm in the face of serious scientific debate and careful evaluation of alternative explanations.”
      </p>
      <div class="templatequotecite">
       <cite>
        —
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council" title="United States National Research Council">
         United States National Research Council
        </a>
        ,
        <i>
         Advancing the Science of Climate Change
        </i>
       </cite>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Of most concern in these anthropogenic factors is the increase in CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      levels due to emissions from
      <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">
       fossil fuel
      </a>
      combustion, followed by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Particulate" title="Particulate">
       aerosols
      </a>
      (particulate matter in the atmosphere) and the CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      released by
      <a href="/wiki/Cement" title="Cement">
       cement
      </a>
      manufacture. Other factors, including land use,
      <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">
       ozone depletion
      </a>
      , animal agriculture
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steinfeld2006_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Steinfeld2006-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">
       deforestation
      </a>
      , are also of concern in the roles they play – both separately and in conjunction with other factors – in affecting climate,
      <a href="/wiki/Microclimate" title="Microclimate">
       microclimate
      </a>
      , and measures of climate variables.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20151128_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151128-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_evidence">
       Physical evidence
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="center">
      <div class="thumb tnone">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:16-008-NASA-2015RecordWarmGlobalYearSince1880-20160120.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/16-008-NASA-2015RecordWarmGlobalYearSince1880-20160120.png/500px-16-008-NASA-2015RecordWarmGlobalYearSince1880-20160120.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/16-008-NASA-2015RecordWarmGlobalYearSince1880-20160120.png/750px-16-008-NASA-2015RecordWarmGlobalYearSince1880-20160120.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/16-008-NASA-2015RecordWarmGlobalYearSince1880-20160120.png/1000px-16-008-NASA-2015RecordWarmGlobalYearSince1880-20160120.png 2x" width="500"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:16-008-NASA-2015RecordWarmGlobalYearSince1880-20160120.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <center>
          2015 –
          <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
           Warmest Global Year
          </a>
          on Record (since 1880) – Colors indicate temperature anomalies (
          <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
           NASA
          </a>
          /
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NOAA" title="NOAA">
           NOAA
          </a>
          ; 20 January 2016).
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20160120_64-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20160120-64">
            [64]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </center>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NSFmonsoonsandclimatesince200AD.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1908" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/NSFmonsoonsandclimatesince200AD.jpg/250px-NSFmonsoonsandclimatesince200AD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/NSFmonsoonsandclimatesince200AD.jpg/375px-NSFmonsoonsandclimatesince200AD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/NSFmonsoonsandclimatesince200AD.jpg/500px-NSFmonsoonsandclimatesince200AD.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NSFmonsoonsandclimatesince200AD.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Comparisons between Asian
        <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">
         Monsoons
        </a>
        from 200
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AD" title="AD">
         AD
        </a>
        to 2000 AD (staying in the background on other plots), Northern Hemisphere temperature, Alpine glacier extent (vertically inverted as marked), and human history as noted by the U.S.
        <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">
         NSF
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASAarctic_temp_trends_rt.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="389" data-file-width="574" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/NASAarctic_temp_trends_rt.jpg/250px-NASAarctic_temp_trends_rt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/NASAarctic_temp_trends_rt.jpg/375px-NASAarctic_temp_trends_rt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/NASAarctic_temp_trends_rt.jpg/500px-NASAarctic_temp_trends_rt.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NASAarctic_temp_trends_rt.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Arctic temperature anomalies over a 100-year period as estimated by
        <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
         NASA
        </a>
        . Typical high monthly variance can be seen, while longer-term averages highlight trends.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Evidence for climatic change is taken from a variety of sources that can be used to reconstruct past climates. Reasonably complete global records of surface temperature are available beginning from the mid-late 19th century. For earlier periods, most of the evidence is indirect—climatic changes are inferred from changes in
      <a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">
       proxies
      </a>
      , indicators that reflect climate, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Vegetation" title="Vegetation">
       vegetation
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">
       ice cores
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Dendrochronology" title="Dendrochronology">
       dendrochronology
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_level_change" title="Sea level change">
       sea level change
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glacial_geology" title="Glacial geology">
       glacial geology
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature_measurements_and_proxies">
       Temperature measurements and proxies
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_temperature_record" title="Instrumental temperature record">
       instrumental temperature record
      </a>
      from surface stations was supplemented by
      <a href="/wiki/Radiosonde" title="Radiosonde">
       radiosonde balloons
      </a>
      , extensive atmospheric monitoring by the mid-20th century, and, from the 1970s on, with
      <a href="/wiki/Satellite_temperature_measurements" title="Satellite temperature measurements">
       global satellite data
      </a>
      as well. The
      <sup>
       18
      </sup>
      O/
      <sup>
       16
      </sup>
      O ratio in calcite and ice core samples
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_isotope_ratio_cycle" title="Oxygen isotope ratio cycle">
       used to deduce ocean temperature in the distant past
      </a>
      is an example of a temperature proxy method, as are other climate metrics noted in subsequent categories.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_and_archaeological_evidence">
       Historical and archaeological evidence
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Historical_impacts_of_climate_change" title="Historical impacts of climate change">
       Historical impacts of climate change
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Climate change in the recent past may be detected by corresponding changes in settlement and agricultural patterns.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demenocal01_66-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Demenocal01-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeological" title="Archaeological">
       Archaeological
      </a>
      evidence,
      <a href="/wiki/Oral_history" title="Oral history">
       oral history
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historical_documents" title="Historical documents">
       historical documents
      </a>
      can offer insights into past changes in the climate. Climate change effects have been linked to the collapse of various civilizations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demenocal01_66-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Demenocal01-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glacier_Mass_Balance.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="658" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Glacier_Mass_Balance.png/250px-Glacier_Mass_Balance.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Glacier_Mass_Balance.png/375px-Glacier_Mass_Balance.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Glacier_Mass_Balance.png/500px-Glacier_Mass_Balance.png 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glacier_Mass_Balance.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Decline in thickness of glaciers worldwide over the past half-century
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Glaciers">
       Glaciers
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">
       Glaciers
      </a>
      are considered among the most sensitive indicators of climate change.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seiz2007_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Seiz2007-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Their size is determined by a
      <a href="/wiki/Mass_balance" title="Mass balance">
       mass balance
      </a>
      between snow input and melt output. As temperatures warm, glaciers retreat unless snow precipitation increases to make up for the additional melt; the converse is also true.
     </p>
     <p>
      Glaciers grow and shrink due both to natural variability and external forcings. Variability in temperature, precipitation, and englacial and subglacial hydrology can strongly determine the evolution of a glacier in a particular season. Therefore, one must average over a decadal or longer time-scale and/or over many individual glaciers to smooth out the local short-term variability and obtain a glacier history that is related to climate.
     </p>
     <p>
      A world glacier inventory has been compiled since the 1970s, initially based mainly on aerial photographs and maps but now relying more on satellites. This compilation tracks more than 100,000 glaciers covering a total area of approximately 240,000 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      , and preliminary estimates indicate that the remaining ice cover is around 445,000 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      . The World Glacier Monitoring Service collects data annually on
      <a href="/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850" title="Retreat of glaciers since 1850">
       glacier retreat
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Glacier_mass_balance" title="Glacier mass balance">
       glacier mass balance
      </a>
      . From this data, glaciers worldwide have been found to be shrinking significantly, with strong glacier retreats in the 1940s, stable or growing conditions during the 1920s and 1970s, and again retreating from the mid-1980s to present.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zemp2008_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Zemp2008-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The most significant climate processes since the middle to late
      <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">
       Pliocene
      </a>
      (approximately 3 million years ago) are the glacial and
      <a href="/wiki/Interglacial" title="Interglacial">
       interglacial
      </a>
      cycles. The present interglacial period (the
      <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">
       Holocene
      </a>
      ) has lasted about 11,700 years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICS2008_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ICS2008-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Shaped by
      <a href="/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles" title="Milankovitch cycles">
       orbital variations
      </a>
      , responses such as the rise and fall of
      <a href="/wiki/Continental_climate" title="Continental climate">
       continental
      </a>
      ice sheets and significant sea-level changes helped create the climate. Other changes, including
      <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_event" title="Heinrich event">
       Heinrich events
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Dansgaard%E2%80%93Oeschger_event" title="Dansgaard–Oeschger event">
       Dansgaard–Oeschger events
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Younger_Dryas" title="Younger Dryas">
       Younger Dryas
      </a>
      , however, illustrate how glacial variations may also influence climate without the
      <a href="/wiki/Orbital_forcing" title="Orbital forcing">
       orbital forcing
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Glaciers leave behind
      <a href="/wiki/Moraine" title="Moraine">
       moraines
      </a>
      that contain a wealth of material—including organic matter, quartz, and potassium that may be dated—recording the periods in which a glacier advanced and retreated. Similarly, by
      <a href="/wiki/Tephrochronology" title="Tephrochronology">
       tephrochronological
      </a>
      techniques, the lack of glacier cover can be identified by the presence of soil or volcanic
      <a href="/wiki/Tephra" title="Tephra">
       tephra
      </a>
      horizons whose date of deposit may also be ascertained.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Arctic_sea_ice_loss">
       Arctic sea ice loss
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Arctic_sea_ice_decline" title="Arctic sea ice decline">
       Arctic sea ice decline
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_the_Arctic" title="Climate change in the Arctic">
       Climate change in the Arctic
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The decline in Arctic sea ice, both in extent and thickness, over the last several decades is further evidence for rapid climate change.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sea ice is frozen seawater that floats on the ocean surface. It covers millions of square miles in the polar regions, varying with the seasons. In the
      <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">
       Arctic
      </a>
      , some sea ice remains year after year, whereas almost all
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">
       Southern Ocean
      </a>
      or Antarctic sea ice melts away and reforms annually. Satellite observations show that Arctic sea ice is now declining at a rate of 13.3 percent per decade, relative to the 1981 to 2010 average.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:512px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Plant_Productivity_in_a_Warming_World.ogv/512px--Plant_Productivity_in_a_Warming_World.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:512px;height:288px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="213.53070294785" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Plant_Productivity_in_a_Warming_World.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/Plant_Productivity_in_a_Warming_World.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 512 × 288 (656 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="512" data-height="288" data-bandwidth="656163" data-framerate="29.96999931427"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/2e/Plant_Productivity_in_a_Warming_World.ogv/Plant_Productivity_in_a_Warming_World.ogv.160p.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth Ogg video (160P)" data-shorttitle="Ogg 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.ogv" data-width="284" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="194472" data-framerate="15"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'>
