         <span class="play-btn-large">
          <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
           Play media
          </span>
         </span>
        </a>
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plant_Productivity_in_a_Warming_World.ogv" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        This video summarizes how climate change, associated with increased carbon dioxide levels, has affected plant growth.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Vegetation">
       Vegetation
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A change in the type, distribution and coverage of vegetation may occur given a change in the climate. Some changes in climate may result in increased precipitation and warmth, resulting in improved plant growth and the subsequent sequestration of airborne CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      . A gradual increase in warmth in a region will lead to earlier flowering and fruiting times, driving a change in the timing of life cycles of dependent organisms. Conversely, cold will cause plant bio-cycles to lag.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Larger, faster or more radical changes, however, may result in vegetation stress, rapid plant loss and desertification in certain circumstances.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse_73-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse-73">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
      An example of this occurred during the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carboniferous_Rainforest_Collapse" title="Carboniferous Rainforest Collapse">
       Carboniferous Rainforest Collapse
      </a>
      (CRC), an extinction event 300 million years ago. At this time vast rainforests covered the equatorial region of Europe and America. Climate change devastated these tropical rainforests, abruptly fragmenting the habitat into isolated 'islands' and causing the extinction of many plant and animal species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse_73-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse-73">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pollen_analysis">
       Pollen analysis
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Palynology" title="Palynology">
       Palynology
      </a>
      is the study of contemporary and fossil
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palynomorph" title="Palynomorph">
       palynomorphs
      </a>
      , including
      <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">
       pollen
      </a>
      . Palynology is used to infer the geographical distribution of plant species, which vary under different climate conditions. Different groups of plants have pollen with distinctive shapes and surface textures, and since the outer surface of pollen is composed of a very resilient material, they resist decay. Changes in the type of pollen found in different layers of sediment in lakes, bogs, or river deltas indicate changes in plant communities. These changes are often a sign of a changing climate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As an example, palynological studies have been used to track changing vegetation patterns throughout the
      <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">
       Quaternary glaciations
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and especially since the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_maximum" title="Last glacial maximum">
       last glacial maximum
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="994" data-file-width="558" height="392" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg/220px-Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg/330px-Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg/440px-Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         Top:
        </i>
        <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">
         Arid
        </a>
        ice age climate
        <div style="clear:both;">
        </div>
        <i>
         Middle:
        </i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic_period" title="Atlantic period">
         Atlantic Period
        </a>
        , warm and wet
        <div style="clear:both;">
        </div>
        <i>
         Bottom:
        </i>
        Potential vegetation in climate now if not for human effects like agriculture.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ORNL_paleoclimate_79-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-ORNL_paleoclimate-79">
          [79]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cloud_cover_and_precipitation">
       Cloud cover and precipitation
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">
       Cloud
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">
       Precipitation
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Past precipitation can be estimated in the modern era with the global network of precipitation gauges. Surface coverage over oceans and remote areas is relatively sparse, but, reducing reliance on
      <a href="/wiki/Interpolation" title="Interpolation">
       interpolation
      </a>
      , satellite clouds and precipitation data has been available since the 1970s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IJC_precip_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IJC_precip-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Quantification of climatological variation of precipitation in prior centuries and epochs is less complete but approximated using proxies such as marine sediments, ice cores, cave stalagmites, and tree rings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In July 2016 scientists published evidence of increased cloud cover over polar regions,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
      as predicted by climate models.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Climatological temperatures substantially affect cloud cover and precipitation. For instance, during the
      <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">
       Last Glacial Maximum
      </a>
      of 18,000 years ago, thermal-driven
      <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">
       evaporation
      </a>
      from the oceans onto continental landmasses was low, causing large areas of extreme desert, including
      <a href="/wiki/Polar_desert" title="Polar desert">
       polar deserts
      </a>
      (cold but with low rates of cloud cover and precipitation).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ORNL_paleoclimate_79-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ORNL_paleoclimate-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In contrast, the world's climate was cloudier and wetter than today near the start of the warm
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic_period" title="Atlantic period">
       Atlantic Period
      </a>
      of 8000 years ago.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ORNL_paleoclimate_79-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ORNL_paleoclimate-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Estimated global land precipitation increased by approximately 2% over the course of the 20th century, though the calculated trend varies if different time endpoints are chosen, complicated by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o-Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño-Southern Oscillation">
       ENSO
      </a>
      and other oscillations, including greater global land cloud cover precipitation in the 1950s and 1970s than the later 1980s and 1990s despite the positive trend over the century overall.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IJC_precip_80-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-IJC_precip-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_watercycletrend_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-USGS_watercycletrend-84">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Similar slight overall increase in global river runoff and in average soil moisture has been perceived.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_watercycletrend_84-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-USGS_watercycletrend-84">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Dendroclimatology">
       Dendroclimatology
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Dendroclimatology" title="Dendroclimatology">
       Dendroclimatology
      </a>
      is the analysis of tree ring growth patterns to determine past climate variations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Wide and thick rings indicate a fertile, well-watered growing period, whilst thin, narrow rings indicate a time of lower rainfall and less-than-ideal growing conditions.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ice_cores">
       Ice cores
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Analysis of ice in a core drilled from an
      <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">
       ice sheet
      </a>
      such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">
       Antarctic ice sheet
      </a>
      , can be used to show a link between temperature and global sea level variations. The air trapped in bubbles in the ice can also reveal the CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      variations of the atmosphere from the distant past, well before modern environmental influences. The study of these ice cores has been a significant indicator of the changes in CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      over many millennia, and continues to provide valuable information about the differences between ancient and modern atmospheric conditions.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">
       Animals
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Remains of
      <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">
       beetles
      </a>
      are common in freshwater and land sediments. Different species of beetles tend to be found under different climatic conditions. Given the extensive lineage of beetles whose genetic makeup has not altered significantly over the millennia, knowledge of the present climatic range of the different species, and the age of the sediments in which remains are found, past climatic conditions may be inferred.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coope1999_87-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Coope1999-87">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Similarly, the historical abundance of various
      <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">
       fish
      </a>
      species has been found to have a substantial relationship with observed climatic conditions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Changes in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_productivity" title="Primary productivity">
       primary productivity
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autotrophs" title="Autotrophs">
       autotrophs
      </a>
      in the oceans can affect marine food webs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_level_change">
       Sea level change
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">
       Sea level
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Current_sea_level_rise" title="Current sea level rise">
       Current sea level rise
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Global sea level change for much of the last century has generally been estimated using
      <a href="/wiki/Tide_gauge" title="Tide gauge">
       tide gauge
      </a>
      measurements collated over long periods of time to give a long-term average. More recently,
      <a href="/wiki/Altimeter" title="Altimeter">
       altimeter
      </a>
      measurements — in combination with accurately determined
      <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">
       satellite
      </a>
      orbits — have provided an improved measurement of global sea level change.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      To measure sea levels prior to instrumental measurements, scientists have dated
      <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">
       coral reefs
      </a>
      that grow near the surface of the ocean, coastal sediments,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_terrace" title="Marine terrace">
       marine terraces
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ooids" title="Ooids">
       ooids
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">
       limestones
      </a>
      , and nearshore archaeological remains. The predominant dating methods used are
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium-thorium_dating" title="Uranium-thorium dating">
       uranium series
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">
       radiocarbon
      </a>
      , with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmogenic_radionuclide_dating" title="Cosmogenic radionuclide dating">
       cosmogenic radionuclides
      </a>
      being sometimes used to date terraces that have experienced relative sea level fall. In the early
      <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">
       Pliocene
      </a>
      , global temperatures were 1–2˚C warmer than the present temperature, yet sea level was 15–25 meters higher than today.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
       Notes
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
      <ol class="references">
       <li id="cite_note-1">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-1">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <cite class="citation book">
          America's Climate Choices: Panel on Advancing the Science of Climate Change; National Research Council (2010).
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.nap.edu/catalog.php?record_id=12782" rel="nofollow">
           <i>
            Advancing the Science of Climate Change
           </i>
          </a>
          . Washington, D.C.: The National Academies Press.
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-309-14588-0" title="Special:BookSources/0-309-14588-0">
           0-309-14588-0
          </a>
          .
          <q>
           (p1) ... there is a strong, credible body of evidence, based on multiple lines of research, documenting that climate is changing and that these changes are in large part caused by human activities. While much remains to be learned, the core phenomenon, scientific questions, and hypotheses have been examined thoroughly and have stood firm in the face of serious scientific debate and careful evaluation of alternative explanations. * * * (pp. 21–22) Some scientific conclusions or theories have been so thoroughly examined and tested, and supported by so many independent observations and results, that their likelihood of subsequently being found to be wrong is vanishingly small. Such conclusions and theories are then regarded as settled facts. This is the case for the conclusions that the Earth system is warming and that much of this warming is very likely due to human activities.
          </q>
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AClimate+change&amp;rft.au=America%27s+Climate+Choices%3A+Panel+on+Advancing+the+Science+of+Climate+Change%3B+National+Research+Council&amp;rft.btitle=Advancing+the+Science+of+Climate+Change&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.nap.edu%2Fcatalog.php%3Frecord_id%3D12782&amp;rft.isbn=0-309-14588-0&amp;rft.place=Washington%2C+D.C.&amp;rft.pub=The+National+Academies+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">

          </a>
          . 21 March 1994.
          <q>
           <i>
            Climate change
           </i>
           means a change of climate which is attributed directly or indirectly to human activity that alters the composition of the global atmosphere and which is in addition to natural climate variability observed over comparable time periods.
          </q>
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AClimate+change&amp;rft.btitle=The+United+Nations+Framework+Convention+on+Climate+Change&amp;rft.date=1994-03-21&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Funfccc.int%2Fessential_background%2Fconvention%2Fbackground%2Fitems%2F1349.php&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">

           <span class="nowrap">
            8 July
           </span>
           2011
          </span>
          .
          <q>
           Climate System: The five physical components (atmosphere, hydrosphere, cryosphere, lithosphere, and biosphere) that are responsible for the climate and its variations.
          </q>
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AClimate+change&amp;rft.btitle=Glossary&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fearthobservatory.nasa.gov%2FGlossary%2Findex.php%3Fmode%3Dalpha%26seg%3Db%26segend%3Dd&amp;rft.pub=NASA+Earth+Observatory&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>

       <li id="cite_note-41">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-41">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <cite class="citation journal" id="CITEREFHaighAnn_R._WinningRalf_ToumiJerald_W._Harder2010">
          Haigh, Joanna D.; Ann R. Winning; Ralf Toumi; Jerald W. Harder (2010-10-07). "An influence of solar spectral variations on radiative forcing of climate".
          <i>
           Nature
          </i>
          .
          <b>
           467
          </b>
          (7316): 696–9.
          <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">
           Bibcode
          </a>
          :
          <a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2010Natur.467..696H" rel="nofollow">
           2010Natur.467..696H
          </a>
          .
          <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">
           doi
          </a>
          :
          <a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1038%2Fnature09426" rel="nofollow">
           10.1038/nature09426
          </a>
          .
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">
           ISSN
          </a>
          <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0028-0836" rel="nofollow">
           0028-0836
          </a>
          .
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier">

          </a>
          <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/20930841" rel="nofollow">
           20930841
          </a>
          .
          <q>
           Currently there is insufficient observational evidence to validate the spectral variations observed by SIM, or to fully characterize other solar cycles, but our findings raise the possibility that the effects of solar variability on temperature throughout the atmosphere may be contrary to current expectations.
          </q>
         </cite>

        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AClimate+change&amp;rft.atitle=A+note+of+the+relationship+between+ice+core+methane+concentrations+and+insolation&amp;rft.au=Schmidt%2C+G.+A.%2C+Shindel%2C+D.+T.+and+Harder%2C+S.&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.agu.org%2Fpubs%2Fcrossref%2F2004%2F2004GL021083.shtml&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2004GeoRL..3123206S&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1029%2F2004GL021083&amp;rft.issue=23&amp;rft.jtitle=Geophys.+Res.+Lett.&amp;rft.pages=L23206&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=31">
         <span style="display:none;">
         </span>
        </span>
        <span class="citation-comment" style="display:none; color:#33aa33">
         CS1 maint: Multiple names: authors list (
         <a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_Multiple_names:_authors_list" title="Category:CS1 maint: Multiple names: authors list">
          link
         </a>
         )
        </span>
       </li>
       <li>
        Wagner, Frederic H., (ed.)
        <i>
         Climate Change in Western North America: Evidence and Environmental Effects
        </i>
        (2009).
        <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780874809060">
